3740 Manito
Fort Wayne, Indiana
May 15, 1.95 7

Dear Miss Bemelmans:

I am so ashamed that I did not answer your nice letter sooner, regarding
your request to get in touch with Congressman Harvey.

I did contact a few people in Indiana, to inquire if he was their representative,
but each time I did not hit his territory.

Our organization in Fort Wayne is at a standstill, and just a few of us
have been carrying the load of work that has come to the attention of our
animal organization. So with working all day, and trying to help
animals on the side, I have neglected some very important phases of
animal work, I know.

I have sinCe heard that Rep. Harvey is for the bill. Is this true?

What I would like to know is: What is the present status of the humane
slaughter bills ? Are they still talking about it in the House subcommittee?

Our paper has not carried any editorial on it this year. But, I am anxious
to get something in both Fort Wayne papers, preferably the editorial
section. Would you mind sending me up to date information, the status of
the hearings, the best timing for an editorial, the best bills Cii’id why,

and any pertinent information for an editorial.

I realize I may be too late. However, the wife of a congressman from
Indianapolis has told me that this is a better time to hit the representatives
than before Easter. So I hope Fort Wayne will not be too late with
editorials .

Also, do you mind sending to me the pertinent names on the committees,
to whom we should write?

I am asking a lot of you, when I was so slow in my reply to you. However,
things have worked outnow so that I can start working on the slaughter part
of humane work. ‘

It was so nice of you to write to me and to think of me in trying to get help.
I can honestly tell you that if It I had not had such a load in the past several
weeks, you would have heard from me right away. I am always so willing
to be of help.

SOCIETY FOR ANIMAL PROTECTIVE LEGISLATION
745 FIFTH AVENUE. ROOM 812
NEW YORK 22, NEW YORK

; June 10, 1957

Miss Mary A. Gilliam
SPCA, BQX'll7h
Petersburg,‘Virginia

Dear Miss Gilliam:

Knowing of the excellent and active work you have been
doing for enactment of an effective humane slaughter law,
I am.writing to ask for your help in learning how each of
the Representatives from Virginia stands on the subjects
I know that the Hon. Watkins M. Abbitt from.your district
is in favor of it, and I understand that the Hon. Joel T.
Broyhill is too. Any information you could send about the
others would be much appreciated. I am sure that you will
do everything possible to encourage any'who are not yet
decided to support the measure.

‘With kind regards and good wishes,

Sincerely,

diagé;

Secretary-Treasurer

Dear mrs. Stevens,

Please excuse me for using your letter for a reply, but all my our
stationery is used up and I have left plain paper at home. Thought
you would rather hear promptly than wait for me to get it.

Senator Byrd says definitely in his letters that he will vote for the
humane slanghter bills before Congress when they come up. Senator

A. Willis Robertson he will take our requests into consideration
when the bills come ups He has never said whether or not he would
vote for them, as far as I can find out. I surely hope the law will
he passed this time; I am sorry I have not been able to do as much
about it this time and have had to give up working at home at night.
It looks like I am coming to the end of my rope. However, we sent
the folder with the poor pig being sliced into to meetings of

the Kiwanis Lions and Rotor Clubs in Peterstr' a d to the ions
and Optimist Clubs in Colonigl Heights: but didn‘t have enoug

to the Senior Club, both with short letters attached. We had enough of these. If the
bills are net passed this time, which Heaven fonfend, I'll be glad to send whatever new
folders you can send. 'Wg have had two letters in the newspaper, but the Editor will
not write another editarial for some time, I am afraid. Hewever, I don't think we can
complain. The Progress-Index has been very genercus with us and has printed everything
we have esked them to during the whole time. I also sent cut a news release and I
believe a second ene, to all newspapers, radia and TV Statiens in GUr district, and

it when he gave out notices. Felders with letters were sent to all members at our
Annual Meeting , too. Its not much, I knew, and I am sorry I couldn't de unre. 1he
members of the Board wrote many letters in the beginning, and a few later on, and
a*yeung girl whe is interested in animals wrote a great many letters for ether people

It leeks like this and the millians ef letters from other places eught to more these

} To The Editor:

1 We’ve been reading a great
deal recently from Humane So-

ciety agitators regarding “hu- v

mane” slaughtering of meat. 1
If we are going to eat méat

at all, our prime goal should

be to procure meat that is un—
. adulterated with chemicals,‘

hormones and anesthetics» We

should be more interested in our, ;

own health than in ‘ the com-

'fort ‘ of the animal as it
~ Capproaches slaughter.

. For all we knOw the animal
-might' be just as frightened
, when it enters the gas chamber
_ as when it meets the knife.
The meat we buy is already
contaminated with growth sti-
mulates which were fed to it
' months ago. Why add anything
. more? ' ‘ . '

AIDA SMITH; _

NEW JERSEY COMMITTEE FOR HUMANE SLAUGHTER OF MEAT ANIMALS

58 Church Street
Bloomfields N. J.
May 6, 1957

To the Editor

Organizations and individuals who have supported compulsory slaughter
reform so effectively should now be on their guard against a compromise bill
that spells defeato On April 23 this Senate bill” So 12139 received the full support
of the Department of Agriculture.

Secretary Ezra Taft Benson stated that he favors this bill because it
”promotesH rather than ”requires”S and provides ”an orderly approach to humane
slaughter"o There would be cooperation between the packers9 humane organiza-
tions and the Department of Agriculture.

Under this bill the situation in this country would remain exactly as it
is and has been for 28 yearss except that the cooperation would include a govern-
ment department, Let us look at the record. Our packing industry formed a
committee 28 years ago to cooperate with humane organizations on slaughter
methods, This committee is still activeo The total result to date is that not more
than one in a hundred of our packers operates under humane slaughtero It is
evident that cooperation has been tried and found wanting,

In contrast let us look at Western Europe, We find 8 countries and 100
million people under compulsory humane slaughtero for over 20 years, and with
methods and instruments already developed and in use.

In our country also9 without assistance from taxpayers money9 we have

invented and a small minority of our packers are using carbon dioxide anesthesia

 




To the Editor 5/6/57

' s

and the Remington Arms stunner9 as well as the European methodso American
industry does not need an act of Congress to assist it in improving its methods
once it is faced with a requirement to act,

With this record in minds, may we ask why humane slaughter should not be
required within a reasonable time for all interstate packers ? Two years of
preparation are allowed in the bills before Congress, How many more years would
the department need for further research” for further cooperation? And if the
department should finally conclude what methods are best, what obligation would
the packers have to use them?

It may not be stretching things too far to suggest that Secretary Benson's
substitute for compulsory humane slaughter would in effect delay this reform for
many years; and that the department's bill is tantamount to voting down humane

slaughter without casting a vote against it,

It is now apparent that a slaughter reform bill of some kind will be passed

by this Congressa and further that the issue is out in the open, Organizations and

individuals of good will who want actual reform9 rather than continued delay, will

so inform their Senators and Representatives before it is too late,

We A0 Bender9 Essex County Member

New Jersey Committee for Humane Slaughter

To the Active Workers for Humane Slaughter in Essex County:

This letter is going to all the County papers and 2 New York papers. Some
may not publish its, and I thought it best to send it to all of you who are in this fight in earnest.

In my opinion our hardest battle is from now on,

Now is the time to write again to our Senators and your Representative. Let
them know that you want a real bill passed and not something to cover for further delay,
”cooperation", and study, It is thumbs down for So 1213.,

 

Sincerely yours,
W0 A. Bender

By the United Press.

CHICAGO. April 29. -- A
spokesman for the American

Meat InStitute said today the
consumer will pay if legisla
tion is passed to provide “more.
humane" slaughter of live-
stock.

There are presently a half-
dozen slaughter bills pending
before House and Senate agri-
culture committees which say
present methods of slaughter
are “cruel.”

The bills are being pushed
by several humane groups who
feel that some European meth-
ods of slaughter should be
used by meat packers in this
country.

Differs From. Europe.

The livestock industry in
Europe is entirely different
from that in the U.S., the AMI
said.

In Europe about one-third as

 

 

l eral

many animals are slaughtered
as in this country, so the prob—
lem is not the same as ours,
the official. said.

Here, meat: production is a
volume business. The spokes
man added, and in Europe it
is not as. much. so.

Each month packing houses
have millions of animals to le.
killed. he said. They can’t let
the animals just stand around,
the AMI said. The slaughtering
must be done in a hurry.

Packers are using the best
methods they know, he added.

The packers must be practical
the AMI said, and many of the
suggested methods of killing to
come out of the Washington
hearings are not practical.

Reasons for Opposition.

Electric shock, he reported,
is a popular European. method
of slaughtering. It is forbid-
den in this country because fed-

'Hu'rnane' Slaughtering Costly
‘And lmprac’rical, Meat Men Say

rfound it is impossible to tell if
an animal was diseased before
death after shock has been
used, he 'said.

Other methods make some of
the meat unfit to eat, the AMI
said.

Here is where the higher
prices will ultimately show up.
the spokesman said. Much
meat is ruined in the shock
method and some of. the “chem-
ical deaths” proposed by these
humane groups.

The AMI official said the
meat industry has been experi-
menting for years with ways '
to make livestock killing easier.

Legislation will not solve the
problems, the AMI asserted,
but added that research will.

Many companies currently
are experimenting with new
chemical methods of stunning
before death, others are develv
oping new firearms for slaughv

 

meat inspectors have

1505 Potomac Street, N.W;,
Washington 7, D.C.
May 14, 1957.

Mrs. Madeleine Bemelmans,

% Society for Animal Protective hegislation.
745 Fifth_Avenue,

Ken'iork 22, N.Y. Boon 812

My dear Mrs. Bemelmans:

It was good to get your letter. I am sending you copies of some
of the hills which have been sent to me.

One of the weapons we have is the boycott and l propose to buy
whenever possible only Hormel and Kingan meats since these two

are the only firms willing to install humane slaughtering equipment.
Since I am also a member of the Housewives United (would you like

to join us or are you a.member ~ I do not have access to the membership
list) and propose to discuss the matter with them next week at a

Tea we are having. I have lived in almost every state of the union
and have friends and relatives scattered all over and am writing them
to join me in the boycott. It seems to me to be the only weapon we
have against entrenched greed and inhumanity. 1 am thinking of the
huge advertising campaign being put on currently by Swift...I'd be
much more impressed if they could advertise that they were installing
the most humane methods of slaughter instead of knowing that they
are fighting this legislation and lobbying against it.

The "Mr. Prince" i mentioned was written Up in LIFE a couple of
years back and he instigated more humane treatment of the animals
BEFORE slaughter such as the elimination of the electric prodding rod
and a better ramp for the hogs...I dannot remember but i think he

was with Armour..seems he inherited the business from an Uncle. I’d
like some may of ascertaining accurately but do not have time to

go to-the library and go through odd files..amaybe you could 8phone
them there in New lork (Life, that is) and find out. “

I do so hope we are able to force this legislation on the packers...
I'd like better sanitary inspection too.

‘. Sincerely,l~ Ixég;
77 ‘ . (2M £17.. “/2
(Mr . L. M. Gary)

cc: Senator Hubert Humphrey

Cay Carrier 8 DeKoIb Place Morrisfown, New Jersey

   

April 28, 1957

 

Mrs. Christine Stevens’
Secretary-Treasurer
.Animal Protective Legislation
745 Fifth.Avenue

New Ybrk, N. Y.

 
   
     
   
  
  
 

 

Stevens:

 

Dear Mrs.

Thank you for the brochure on humane slaughter bills,
and the list of committeemen. I thought your leaflet the
.most comprehensive and useful I have seen yet on.the sub-
ject. I will certainly write all the committeemen.that

I can.

      
      
  
 

   

I already have written Hon. W. R. Poage on the subject,
and received an acknowledgement.

    
 
 
 
      
        
  
 
    
  
 
 
 
   
     
     
   

 
 

I have done quite a bit of publicity in Jersey for
bill A106, Humane Education in schools, and a few weeks
ago both Henrietta Hitchcock of Cats and their Care in.the
World Telegram, and Will Hendrix of the Daily News devoted
very kindly their entire column.to supporting this Jersey
bill. I also enclose a letter I get published in the
Nevark News which is partly on Humane Slaughter legislation.
We are still fighting a battle here for A106.

Do you think it possible to get more of the big
daily papers to write editorials in behalf of humane
bills, or have you tried it? You seldom see anything
in the editorial departments on animal welfare. Let me
know - if you can.

The unwillingness of legislators to do anything for
animals, and the indifference of the meat-glutting public
to the terrible, insane cruelty to animals in the slaughter
pens is a great degradation to the human race. No wonder
they don‘t treat fellow members of the human race much
better than they do.

Yours sincerely,

/:/ Me ﬂ ﬂ pm Mb if"?

P.S. Please mail me six additional copies of the .
brochure and list of committeemen. If it is agreeable
to you, I will see if I can reach.some local editors
on the subject.

Also, you might send about 10 of the above two to ,
(over) '




Mrs. Christiana Bertram,
Humane Education.Committee
167 Park Place,
Tenafly, New Jersey.

in ﬂhe event you have not already written her. She is very
interested in the Washington legislation. Mrs. Bertram is
the "first lady of animal Welfare" in Jersey, and has a very
active group which I help. I am sure she oculd get at least

ten of her people to write.

I hope this helps a bit.

Humane Sunday sermon
by the
Reverend Lloyd Putnam

 

EN LARGING
OUR
RELIGION

 




Should we be concerned about en—
larging our religion? That question
may sound odd since our religion pro-
bably already includes a great many
things and covers a lot of territory. It
probably deals with our relationship to

IVhy should a man concern himself i
This question and its answers are at i
movement. Many people who recog

J Q.

God and to our fellowmen and that means

their fellow men see no obligation toze
a religion of wide scope. But are there

have not seen Creation as a whole. I)

still some ways inwhich our faith should . . . H
minister answers the haszc why” fog

be expanded, enlarged, deepened, and
intensified if it is to be true to Biblical
insights ‘? A religion canbecome alltoo
humanly—centered and Humane Sunday
gives us a good Opportunity to raise
some basic questions about the sc0pe of
our faith.

some wonder of God's creation. A fa—
vorite trick of magicians is to take a
live rabbit, put it in a black box, and
then slowly "grind" the rabbit througha
wringer attached to the box. Ofcourse,
all that appears is a limp, cardboard
replica of a rabbit! To the delight of
the audience, the magician eventually
manages to reproduce the rabbit - not
dead but very much alive.

First let us ask if our View of God
and His creation is too small? Does it
need to be enlarged? A world famous
evangelist recently found himself in
trouble. He was in England and started
a radio broadcast with these words:
"Fishes belong to the sea, animals be-
long to the jungle, human beings belong
to God. “ The protest was immediate
with one man voicing the sentiments of
many when he said, "Animals are God's
creatures, too. "

Contemporary, scientific man has
played the role of a demonic magician
who has only half-finished the trick.
He has taken the world with all of its
creativity and animal life and has stuff—
ed it into a box onlyto “grind" it out as

A central affirmation of our Chris— a dead, flat, limp, wonder-less world.
tian faith is that human beings belong to The tragedy is that he has mistaken this
God. No one would dispute that, but if replica for the real world; he cannot
that is taken to mean that fishes and appreciate GOd'S sustaining creativity
animals do not belong to Godsomething in all of life because the world is cold
is wrong with our total View of God and and static andlifeless. His View Of God
His world. “Inthe beginning Godcreated is “’9 small- If he will Open his EYES
the heavens and the earth. .. Thus does and ears to all animate life he will dis—-
Genesis express the Hebrew faith that cover aworld that is the result of aGod
the foundation of all of life is God. The in Whom all living things live, move,
fishes and the animals also belong to and have their being.

God - the same God to whom man be—

longs. God in His good purpose has IS our sense of responsibility too

created not only man but the beasts and limited? That is a second question
the birds as well. The God of the Bible worth considering;- Since we oftenfor—
is the creator and sustainer of all of get that GOd is behind 3110f creation,
life. If anyone doubts that, let him—Fead we are tempted to think that 0111' respon—
the first twochapters of Genesis, or the sibility is limited to the human realm
Psalms, especially Psalm 104. These 801813“ BUt do we need to enlarge the
passages wi-llmerely highlight the theme area Of our responsibility?

that runs thr’Oughout the Biblical drama: -

God isthe 'greajt designer back of the As a part Of COSmiC creation God

whole natural order. Not only man but created man in his own image. That
nature and the animal world are seen as has US1.1allybee13, taken to mean that God
.manifesting Godls power and bearing created man with freedom: freedom to

witness to His greatness. choose the right and the wrong, the good

and the'bad. By giving man this power
How easy it is for modern man in of freedom, God gave man "dominion"

his industrial society to miss the awe- over all other living things on earth.

 




iself icith the welfare of animals?
Pe at the very roots of the humane
recognize a moral obligation to
m toward ”lower” creatures. They
71e. In this article a young Baptist
y” for Christians.

Unfortunately, man has not always seen
that a sense of reSponsibility toward all
creatures is also required of him. His
"dominion" is not to be a brutal and
merciless one.

We are called of God to use our
freedomwisely. We canchoosetoabuse
and waste and hurt animal life. But if
we see God’s handiwork in all of creation
we will want to use our freedom to con—
serve, to appreciate, to protect all liv-
ing things. If we really believe that God
is the ruler of the whole universe, we
willwant to make sure that our sense of
responsibility, our sense of steward—
ship, is also universal in scope.

There is an oldlegend about awise
man who could answer any question.
One day a young man approached him
with his hands closed. Hoping to trick
the wise man he asked: "Tell me, sir,
is the bird in my hands alifre or dead? "
The wizard knew that if he said dead,
the man would open his hands and release
the bird into the skies. He also knew
that if he said alive, the man would
crush the bird in his hands. What was
he to reply? He looked at the young
man and said, "The answer to your ques-
tion is in your hands, "

All of us have our freedom and we
can use it to destroy or protect God's
creatures. The answer is up to us.

Realizing our reSponsibility we will
want to raise a last question: Is our love
and concern for life too narrow? Im-
portant as is the realm of human love,
we have a small religion if it has no
room for the rest of God's creations.

Dr. Albert Schweitzer, the medical
missionary in Africa who has become

world famous because of his dedicated
life, has been one of the most persistent
voices in calling men to a deeper, en-
larged sense of love. To him all life
is sacred and thus we should show rev-
erence for all of life. He feels that
sympathy for animals is implicit in the
Christian commandment of love and
explicit in Paul's stress on the redemp—

tion of the whole creation in the eighth
chapter of Romans.

In Schweitzer's View, man should
try as best he can to avert all life from
suffering and destruction. sympathy for
animal life, far from being sentimen-
tality, is to hima duty which no thinking
man can escape. He calls for nothing
short of a “revolution" in ethics in which
past anthrOpocentric concerns will be
complemented by an ethic of boundless
love for all creation.

Is our love too limited or does it
truly extend to the animal realm? One
way to check our answer to that ques—
tion would be to see if we have put our
love into action in the animalkingdom?
Have We helped directly or indirectly
to relieve animal suffering in the past
year ? Have we helped to alert author—
ities when cruelty was taking place and ’
we noticed it? There is a limit to what
one individual can do in the field of hu-
mane work. Fortunately we can join
with many others in our country so that
effective work might be done on a united,
national scale. In spite of recent pro—
gress, the American Humane Associ-
ation can always use more supporters.
There is much work yet to be done! One
way to show that our love surrounds the
animal world as well as the human world
would be to encourage and back the work
of our local humane society.

May 5 to May 11, 1957 is being ob-
served as Be Kind to Animals Week. As
Christians it is a fitting time to see if
we are suffering from religious myopia.
IS our View of God and His creation too
small? Is our sense of responsibility
too limited? Is our love too narrow?
Ifwe must honestly answer in the nega-
tive, let us enlarge our religion that we
may do our part in redeeming all of

creation.

 




 

 

LIAISON

A dog can be a bridge to understanding
Of all our friends in whatsoever form;
We find our love and sympathy expanding

To embrace all creatures that were ever born“

For who are we, but by God's grace were they:

Startled beasts who fear the wiles of man!
It‘s well to love all kinds of life today

And share with them in everything we can;

Who gave to them to know both heat and; cold,
And quick response to earthly love and pain,
Will surely judge the trusts of life we hold

When unto Him He gathers all again.

John E. H. Randerson

THE AMERICAN HUMANE ASSOCIATION
896 PENNSYLVANIA 51'.
DENVER 3, GOLD.

April 26, 1957

7MB Fifth Avenue, Room 812

Gentlemen:

I have received your letter of April Euth
and the leaflet giving information.

It is my desire to send telegrams to several
members of the House Agriculture Committee; in fact
to all the Southern members and expect to write
letters to others.

Your leaflet mentions several bills now
pending such as H.R. 176, H.R. 2880, H.R. 3029,
H.R. 30h9, H.R. 5671, and S. 1&97. I am afraid
I need a little coaching on the wording of the
telegrams. For example, would a telegram such as
the following be worthwhile?

"Hon. Harold D. Cooley, Chairman
House Agriculture Committee
House Office Building
Washington, D. C.

I request your consideration and support of
humane slaughtering bills now pending:

H.R. 176, H.R. 2880, H.R. 2039, H.R. 30h9,
H.R. 5671, and S. 1&97. It is urgent that
compulsory humane slaughter legislation be
enacted in 1957.“

Natural Food Associates is a national organization to help you get the kind of food you
must have to maintain good health.

Natural Food Associates is a non-profit organization with the following objectives:
1. To educate all people to the values of poison-free natural food grown on fertile soil.
2. To tell them how and where to get this food.

February 23, 1957
Natural Food Associates
Atlanta, Texas
Dear Editor:

At our recent National, Convention of Natural Food
Associates at Little Rock, Arkansas, the organization hon-
ored me for the support I had given by giving me a rising
vote of thanks and singing the “Eyes of Texas Are Upon
You.”

As I heard the many speakers and realized the time
and effort many of them had put forth in an endeavor
to help the people of our nation and the world it made
me feel very humble and I felt I was being much too
highly exalted, for I feel it was only through the guid-
ance of “the Great Creator of our Universe,” with the
assistance of Natural Food Associates I was able to accom-
plish the little I have done. ,

Outside the churches of America, I feel Natural Food
Associates is the one organization most nearly endeavor-
ing to carry out the desire of our Lord, when he intreated
us to pray “Our Father which art in heaven, Hallowed
be thy name. Thy kingdom come. Thy will be done in
earth, as it is in heaven.”

In an endeavor to get people to our recent conven-
tion, who I thought would be of most help to our organi-
zation, I invited friends from two major cities nearest
me, and a friend from each of these accepted the invi-
tation, along with our own County Home Demonstration
Agent, but these three were unable to attend on account
of illness.

I invited the Assistant Superintendent of Public
Schools, who acts as health nurse and whom I knew had
the children at heart, to attend the convention. She
attended through the encouragement of the County Su-
perintendent of Schools, the County Health Officer, and
her good husband. I sincerely believe that through the
influence of this woman great good will be accomplished
for our cause. In fact I believe we have tapped another
N. F. A. (oil) gusher our president told us about at our
recent convention. Wrill each member try to find some

Page 4

more N. F. A. gushers? But please don’t overlook any
pumpers, they are keeping many an oil man in business.
Hoping not to appear selfish, I would be very happy

if we could have a mid-summer meeting at one of the
major cities of our state. However I am sure our Board
of Directors will do everything in their power to advance
our cause as far as possible. May I encourage all local
groups to “plan your work, then work your plans” for
a greater Natural Food Associates.

Sincerely,

RAY STINE

Member of N. F. A.

Petrolia, Texas

t b A 3’:

We saw the Natural Foods Magazine in our city
library. We really enjoyed it. We brought it home and
our mother read it, too. She really enjoyed its articles
on the natural foods. The magazine has very interesting
contents and we are so grateful to the lady who made it
possible for us to read this magazine and find out so many
things about food, etc., which we did not know.

My sister and I both want to thank her so much for
sending such a wonderful gift to our library where we,
and others can all study and learn about these things. I’m
sure that many people, especially the young folks, will
be interested in this magazine when they find out about it.

Again, we thank you and the other lady for making

the Natural Foods Magazine available to us and others!

M. B. and M. B.

P.S.——We were the first to take this magazine from the
library. The librarian is very glad to have the
magazine, too.

EDITOR’S NOTE: Natural Food and Farming should be
in every library in the country.
NEWS ITEM

The Columbus, Ohio, N. F. A. Chapter holds regular
meetings once each month, with interesting programs at
each meeting. A dinner is planned in April and the film,
“Gateway To Health,” will be shown. Picnics are plan-
ned for the summer months and these will be held in the
shelter house in the Columbus Rose Garden. Contact
Josephine Kuechle, 2528 Westmont Blvd., in Columbus
for information about these meetings. If you are in the

Columbus area plan to attend.

CORRECTION

One name to be added to the list that furnished food
for the 1957 National Convention in Little Rock, is
ORGANIC ACRES of Seffner, Florida. I. G. Chase,

owner, furnished citrus fruit for one of the meals.

3 April 1957

Honorable Donald E. Tewee
House Agriculture Committee
House Office Building
Washington, D. C.

My dear Mr. Tewes:

Four good oompuleowy humane elaughter bills have been intro~
duoed in the Houee of Representatives. They are: H.R. 3029,
H.R, 176, H,R. 2880 and H.R. 3049,

In an attempt to Stall'paeeage~of the above, Senator w. F.
Bennett and denator A. V. Matkins have introduced 3. 1213.

It only calla for ”study” and ”research". The packers have
been “studying” humane Slaughter for 29 years but only three
paekere have adopted humane methode for even some of the ani-
mals they slaughter. In the-meantime hundreds of millions

of animals die in terror and pain under conditions the average
American would be appalled to witness.

tries have long had humane slaughter legislation, while we
lag behind because of the packers‘ refusal to do anything.

a widespread and powerful dentiment is growing rapidly in the
UoSeao to force packers to adopt-humane slaughter methods.

Konlt you help in thie and give your aupport to H.R. 3029,
30R” 176, Hpﬁa 2880 and ﬁght 3049?

Yours very truly,

C. E. Bogge

March 15, 1957

Mrs. Christine Stevens,
Secretarbereasurer,

Society for Animal Protective Legislation,
745 Fifth Ave, Room 812,

New York 22, N.Y.

Dear mrs. Stevens,

Thank you for your letter of the 26th February. I am
very sorry that we do not have any slaughterhouse photographs other
than those supplied by the American Humane Association, which I am sure
you will already have.

I understand Mr. Stratton has been in touch with you about this
too. As you know, we work very closely together in this matter and in
fact all the photographs we have are in nr. Stratton's possession at the
moment.

The only suggestion I can make would be to take the film produced
by hr. Redman and develop stills from it.

As a matter of fact I would be very grateful indeed if I could
also get hold of some good photographic publicity material on this subject,
since we intend to highlight Humane Slaughter at our exhibit in the
Pacific National Exhibition in Vancouver in August of this year. If by
any chance you obtain photographs from this appeal which you already
possess or if you happen to have any photographs which you can Spare, I
Xould be very grateful if you would lend them to us for the month of
I ugllSto

Yours sincerely,

( T.I.Hughes’
or Executive Director.

1505 Potomac btreet, N.W.,
Washington 7, D.C.
April 4, 1957.

Mrs. Ludwig Bemelmans,
New York, N.Y.

My dear Mrs. Bemelmans:

I do not have your adoress but I did see an item about your efforts
in brew Pearson's column and am asking him to kindly forward this
note to you.

It was gratifying to me to find someone working to change our sadistic
methods of slaughter. i think we ought to congratulate Mr. Prince

in his efforts to treat the animals better before slaughter. I recall
his abolishing the electric prod for the poor beasts. Perhaps we(you
and your grOUp) could seek his cooperation.

May I wish you good luck and offer whatever help I can. i thought

you might like a copy of the letter which I sent as a result of Miss
Free's column. If you could interest other columnist in other states
it might be more effective than just New York...get small energetic
grOUps of women to write their Congressmen!

Sincerely yours, . '

(ﬁgs. L. M. Ga )

 




 

MERCY MEASURE

 

'Congress Shows Interest
In Humane Siaughter

By ANN COTTRELL FREE

Growing political concern over an
issue not often discussed by law-
makers, though it involves ‘both
heart and purse, is shaping up in
Congress

The issue is the extent to which
the Federal Government should
spread its mantle of protection over
animals. Speciﬁcally, should there
be a law providing a more merciful
death for the millions of animals
and fowl sold in interstate com-
merce each year?

On Tuesday, a House agriculture
subcommittee will hear witnesses
and examine ﬁve bills asking for
compulsory humane slaughter. La-
ter, a Senate committee will study
four bills. ’

The compulsory bills all ask that
effective methods be used to make
animals insensible to the thrust
of the knife. They provide for a
two-year compliance period and
exempt slaughter practices of religi-
ous groups, such as Orthodox Jews.

This 1957 congressional drive for
humane slaughter is bi-partisan.
The Department of Agriculture,
however, takes the view of the meat
packers and opposes the proposed
‘ aw.

Avoid Emotional Appeal

Supporters of compulsory legis-
, lation avoid emotion and stick, in-
stead, to facts and ﬁgures. The
‘ chief battleground is economics.
The issue turns on packers willing-
ness to “retool” to modern ways.
Packers freely admit their prime
worry is the expense of installing
anesthetizing equipment. But hu-
mane groups maintain that such
equipment pays for initial expendi-
tures in the long run.

While that dispute continues,
_: these facts emerge:

. 0 Practical methods for humane
‘ dispatch are aVailable.

0 Fifteen countries, all or .in part,
require humane methods.

0 Meat packing accident rate
nearly doubles that of general man-
ufacturing.

0 Meat packers lose millions an-
nually from meat bruised or other-
wise damaged during slaughter.
Economic loss in slaughter houses
'due to injury of man and beast is
unusually high in this country. The
struggle of excited animals often
injures workmen. The Labor De-
partment says that there are 2113
accidents per million manhours in
the meat packing industry com-
, pared to 12.1 in general manu-
', facturing.
1 Injuries to animals are meas-
, ured by losses suffered when bruised
meat is. turned down by inspectors.

Such losses cost packers $16 mil-
lion in 1955.

Few peisons dispute that injury
to man and beast can be elimi-
nated if animals are anesthetized
before being killed. The George A.
.Hormel Co., the ﬁrst to develop
the carbon dioxide‘tunnel, reports
a saving of $50,000 on bruised
hams the ﬁrst year. Also the acci-
dent rate among workers dropped.

During Senate Agriculture Sub-
committee hearings last spring
conducted by Senator Hubert Hum- .
phrey, Democrat of Minnesota, on
his bill, R. W.. Regensburger of
Swift & Co., representing the
American Meat Institute, went to
the heart of the packer’s objec-
tion:

“The cost of installing facili-
ties . . is exceedingly high by
reason of building alterations and
new enclosures to accommodate
the unit.” .

The equipment runs in price from
$3,500 for small units to $39,000
for the largest.

Somewhat less opposition is
voiced over the adoption of sub-
stitutes for the traditional poleaxe
used to knock out beef cattle. Al-
ready about a dozen companies are
using more efﬁcient weapons, such
as the captive 'bolt pistol or a spe-
cial stunning gun developed by
Remington Arms. Packers’ chief
economic worryalong this line has .
not been cost of weapon (prices
range from $100 to $200) but dam-
age to marketable beef brains.

Utah Sidelight

A dramatic political sidelight to
the slaughter issue is being enacted
by three Republican Mormons from

‘Utah. In one corner Representa-

tive William A. Dawson. In the
other, Agriculture Secretary Ezra
Taft Benson and Senator Arthur
V. Watkins.

M1. Dawson introduced a com- '

\ pulsory slaughter bill after several

letters to Secretary Benson. He was
convinced that maintenance of the
status quo was the objective of
both the‘ department and the .
packers.

Mr. Dawson’s bill, along with
the other six introduced this ses-
sion, apparently prompted the in-
troduction of Senator Watkin’s bill.
As pressure for legislation mounted,
Agriculture and the packers real-
ized that their view should be em-
bodied in legislation, too. That
view, as presented in S. 1213 by
Senator Watkins, provides for Ag-
riculture Department-ﬁnanced
study and development of improved
methods. Senator Watkins does
not ask for compulsory adoption
of humane methods, but encour-
ages their acceptance.

Crusading Lady

HUMORIST Ludwig Bemel-
, mans writes books on animals,
such as “Fiﬁ the Poodle.” His
wife attempts to save animals.
She has lined up four Con-
gressmen to help her reform
the brutal slaughtering meth-
ods used by most American
” meatpackers. ' -

William Dawson of Utah .and
Edgar Hiestand of California,
Republicans; and Martha Grif-
ﬁths of Michigan and George
Miller of California, Demo-
crats. All four are backing the
recommendations of the So-
ciety for Animal Protective
Legislation, favoring the adop-
tion of scientiﬁc “mickey
ﬁnns” to make sure, cattle,
hogs, and lambs are fully un-

death blow.

Unfortunately, slaughtering
methods used by most Amerie
can packing h o u s e s. haven’t
changed much since the early
days of the industry. Cattle
are crudely slugged on the
head with sledge hammers,
while pigs are simply lifted up
by one leg in preparation for
the. butcher’s knife. In many
instances the animals are ful-
ly conscious, though painfully
injured, before they die,. and
their screams and squeals are
a nightmare.

Mrs. Bemelmans‘is a quiet

delivers an explosive'rap on

electric shock. All three meth-
‘ods produce sure-ﬁre uncon-

 

The Represent a t i v e s are—

conscious before receiVing the ‘

the animal’s cranium; the car-f
bon dioxide gas chamber; and

sciousness prior to slaughter,

and are used by many Euro-
pean packers. .

Note—Sens. Bennett and
Watkins of Utah may propose
that Congress authorize
“study” of the problem. This
is the time-honored way to
block a proposal that can’t be
fought on its merits.

I am flattered that you remember me from Grand Rapids ~
I certainly didn't make a good showing there, altho I
still feel the sa.me way about the help the little
humane society must have in order to achieve growth.

Our membership has already been alerted to write to

our repres sentatives regarding* support of humane
Slaughter and gave the numbers of the bills to support -
the ones that did not include the provision for an

advisory committee. hi'lmﬂLwli(C9%&Zagth[@me(%%AikhmmmijwaE

to ConxreSS1an Harvey of Indiana. I remember last
year our responses from him to our letters, were quite
unsympathetic. He'll try again, however, if there is
a chance of influencing him. We will advise our
membership.

I don t think there is.much chance of my attending the
hearings - altho I‘ d give my egwe —teeth to do so. You
see, I have a full time job (to earn a livinh) and I
can't get away when I'd like to. That is the only
reason I would like to have money ~ so I could devote
all 01 my time to anmmal welfare - which I love.

I thank you for writing me - write ag ain when youczan.

THE MINISTERS’ CORNER

We have had the great joy of having a
share during the last two months in the
ordinations of two of our Community
Church young men into the liberal ministry.
Both of them received their inspiration to
devote their lives to the service of their
fellowmen from their experiences in this

congregation. They have now set out on 7
the great adventure, having completed their
university and theological school training

in due course.

I wish that all of us might have shared
in these services. I know how all hearts
would have lifted with hope at the sight of j
the faces of these young men, full of ideal- i
ism, courage, anticipation of great days
lying on before, girt for noble encounter
individual ;
lives and ravage society as a whole. There
is no experience more touching than to see 2

with the evils which plague

and hear them accept the responsibilities
that go with ordination, and when the final,
solemn words have been spoken one may
see in their faces the change that comes
with the realization that one’s life is now
given to Truth and God and all Mankind
and will never belong to oneself again.
No matter what may happen to him in
later life, whether he know success or
failure, an ordained minister is a man set
apart, and will be so considered.

This does not mean that the minister is
really different from other people; only
in the completeness of his commitment of
himself to God and his turning of his back
upon self is he different, but this can be
a difference great indeed, and he will feel
its touch upon his soul as long as he draws
his breath.

The Ordination Service includes a ser-
mon, especially pointed to the occasion, a
charge to the young minister concerning his
responsibilities, and to his congregation con-
cerning its, greetings from community, de-
nomination and other churches, and the
solemn rite of ordination in which the peo-
ple of the congregation themselves speak
the sacred words that set their young man
apart.

The two young men whom we have sent
forth into the ministry during these last
two months are Rev. David W. Brown, Uni-
tarian Minister in West Upton, Mass and
Rev. Arnold Thaw, Unitarian Minister in
Natick, Mass. We send them our warmest
congratulations, good wishes and assure
them that all of us at Community Church
will follow their lives and work with inter-
est and affection—DH.

DON’T FORGET

Send in used, good clothing, house-
hold articles, canned goods, books,
etc., for

THE RUMMAGE SALE
June 10, ll, 12

RIGHT NOW
The people of the United States are the

' world’s largest consumers of meat products.

Behind the meticulous preparation of these
foods lies a story of extraordinary cruelty.
The majority of food animals slaughtered in
this country meet a painful end.

The lack of an approved method of hu—
mane slaughter is no longer a valid excuse.
There are various methods by which ani-
mals may be slaughtered humanely. The
instruments are being manufactured at a
price which all slaughter houses can afford.
It is likely that their use may even prove
an economy in the hours saved.

Until recently there was not much that
individuals could do about these conditions.
Now you can make yourself heard through
legislative channels.

Write Now urging the support of HR.
176 Livestock and Poultry: Humane Method
of Slaughtering, sponsored by Hon. Martha
Griffiths to

Representative Harold D. Cooley
House Office Building
Washington, D. C.

The Congressman from your district

Honorable Ezra Taft Benson
Department of Agriculture
Washington Office Building
Washington, D. C.

\ —Social Action Committee

 

YBTJTUU‘“ ct AN BE AN ARTIST"w

YOU are the important factor in the
coming series, “A Creative Experience in
Art”. Led by John Senn'hauser, well-known
artist and a member of Community Church,
the first of three Tuesday evening meetings
will be held on March 12, at 8 P.M., in the
assembly hall.

The session will consist of three parts:
a brief, illustrated introduction to the sub-
ject; a “do-it-yourself” period; and finally,
a critical discussion of what has been pro-
duced by you.

Those participating may buy their own
materials or purchase them inexpensively
at the church that evening. For the first
session, “Experiencing Black and White”,
pencils or charcoal and pad of paper are
suggested. There will be a 75c fee for each
evening.

All ages are urged to attend. Come, and
experience the excitement of creative self-
expression.

THE GREAT AGES

“All centuries are dangerous; it is the
business of the future to be dangerous. It
must be admitted that there is a degree
of instability which is inconsistent with
civilization. But, on the whole, the great
ages have been the unstable ages.”

-Alfred Whitehead

March 7, 1955

Dear Mrs. Stevens,

My husband and I were in N Y recently
and am sorry I wasnt able to get in touch with
you on a Saturday...I did visit Martha Griffiths(DC)
office and the Humane Society of the U S tho(Helen
Jones) in N Y and mr Meyers in D C. Am glad so many
good peeple are working for effective legislation
for animal protection. Also wrote to Kerne wright
of AHA,and recieved a favorable reply.

Attached is a cepy of the letter our Unitarian
FelIOWship is having sent to all Unitarian and
Universalist groups, by courtesy of the Humane Society
of the U S(formerly National Humane Society) who
do an effective job of getting the information to
the public. we have already had several splendid
replies and promises of active backing for humane
slaughter legislation.

I regret that the League of women Voters doesnt
have it on their agenda this year and hope that the
Federation of womens Clubs will give it strong
backing. American Association of University women
should be interested too, as should the churches
of all faiths. we sent a letter to the Lima Council
of churches noting the exemption of "religiously
perscribed methods".A.rabbi of the Reformed Church
here the favors anesthesia for all animals.

Your latest release received and noted.

UNITARIAN FELLOWSHIP Sﬁ‘f‘aﬁxm

0f LIMA, OHIO “m“

Dear Friends:

The unitarian Fellowship of’ldma, Ohio invites you to Join in urging
passage by Congress of a humane animal.slaughter'bill. Hearings have been conducted
before a Senate Committee on a bill sponsored in the last Congress by Senator Humphrey.
A report entitled ”Humane Slaughter" was issued.as Calendar No. 2662. In the Reuse of
REPresentatives Bob Phage of Texas is Chairmen.of a sub-committee which is considering
four similar bills in the Hbuse of Representatives. Last autumn he and other'members
visited packing plants throughout the country to observe methods of animal slaughter.

There is enclosed through the cooperation of the Humane Society of the United
States some background infonmation explaining the prevalent inhumane slaughtering
methods used in all‘but a very few of the USA.plants. It is not pleasant reading, but
it is a."must" for all who believe that the treatment of helpless animals is a measure
of the civilization of a people.

Means and devices are available and have long been in use which painlessly take
the lives of animals to be used as human foods Several European nations have for
many years by law required humane:methods of annual slaughter. In the USA the meat
packing industry has been considering humane slaughtering methods for more than a
quarter of a century without result, except that a few plants have voluntarily adepted
suchlmethods.

We believe that an effective law, operative within a reasonable time, ought to
be enacted without further delay. Such a law will be assured if there is sufficient
expression of public opinion to back the Representatives and Senators who sponsor it.

The Lima Fellowship feels that the combined action of religious liberals may he
a decisive factor in ending a disgraceful period of needless animal suffering.
Therefore, please add the weight of your Church or Fellowship and urge your individual
members to act also. The press, the radio and the pulpit have already spoken out here
and there. Everyone can help by writing to newspapers, periodicals and radio stations;
most directly of all by writing to Representatives and Senators in Congress; par~
ticularly to Senator Hubert Humphrey, to Representative William PoagE, washington,
D. C. and to your National Senators and Representatives whose names can be obtained
from any public library. members of Congress can be addressed simply as:
"Representative", or "Senator" followed by the proper name, ”Washington, D. C."

We hope we may count on you to raise your voice on behalf of‘millions 0f sentient
beings unable to speak for themselves.

Cordially yours,

yWJz mm

.Mrs. Elmer McClain, Secretary

Runneymede Farm, RFD 6

Lima, Ohio

For the Unitarian Fellowship of Lima, Ohio

P.S. We would.be glad to have your suggestions
or to hear of what action your group
takes.

AFFILIATED mm M WIN UNITARMN ASSOCMTRON
MEMBER WESTERN WWARIAN COWSRENJE

 




March 13, 1957

Mr. Paul Maresky
1925 N. Drake St.
Chicago M7, Illinois

Dear Mr. Maresky,

We are interested in obtaining copies of the photographs
showing the hogs crushed in transit, copies of which you sent
Congresswoman Marthe Griffiths and which we see when we
visited her office recently. This cruelty should be stopped,
and such pictures will be helpful in demonstrating the need
for protecting theSe animals in transit.

At. present, we are devoting all of our efforts to
reforming slaughter methods, and would appreciate it very
much if you could send us any photographs of slaughtering
which would be helpful in achieving our purpose.

we shall be glad to reimburse you for the cost of the
photograohs.

Sincerely,

Secretary-Treasurer

MAY 15, 1956

 

 

1026 17th STREET, N. W., WASHINGTON 6, D. 8.

NATIONAL PROGRAM—1956-1958

(As adopted by the national Convention, Chicago, Illinois, April 30—May 4, 1956)

The League of Women Voters, a nonpartisan organization, is dedicated to the principles of self-government established inthe
Constitution of the United States. The League works through its Voters Service and its Program to promote political responSibility
through informed and active participation of citizens in government.

Voters Service is a year-round activity to help the individual citizen to be politically effective. It develops understanding of
the essentials of individual liberty and representative government; it provides nonpartisan factual information on candidates and
issues; it increases citizen participation in political processes in the interest of responSible and responSive government.

CURRENT AGENDA

Governmental issues chosen by the Convention for concerted action.

INDIVIDUAL LIBERTIES: Evaluation of the federal loyalty-security programs, with recognition of the need
for safeguarding national security and protecting individual liberties.

CONSERVATION: Study of water resources.
CONTINUING RESPONSIBILITIES

Positions on national issues to which the League has given sustained attention and on which it may continue to act.

1. U. S. support of the United Nations and its specialized agencies, including adequate ﬁnancial contributions,
increased use, and improved procedures. ,

2. U. S. support of measures designed to keep the peace through the United Nations and regional defense arrange-
ments.

. U. S. support of measures to promote international economic development and technical assistance.

. Opposition to constitutional changes that would limit the existing powers of the Executive and the Congress over
foreign relations.

. Support of U. S. trade policies that will help solve national and international economic problems.

. Measures to improve the budgetary procedures of the Congress.

. Opposition to constitutional limitation on tax rates.
Measures to counteract inﬂation or deﬂation.
Self-government for the District of Columbia; extension of national suffrage to the citizens of the District.

PRINCIPLES

Governmental measures and policies, supported by the League as a whole, which constitute the authorization for adoption of national,
state, territorial, and local Current Agendas.

1. The principles of representative government and individual liberty established in the Constitution of the United
States.

2. A system of government which is responsible to the will of the people and which enables the voter to carry out
his obligations as a citizen.

3. A system of government in which responsibility is clearly ﬁxed.

4. A system of government which promotes coordination of the operations of federal, state, and local governments.

5. Governmental organization and administration which contribute to economy and efficiency.

6. A merit system for the selection, retention, and promotion of government personnel.

‘7. Legal protection of citizens in their right to vote.

8. A system of free public education which provides equal opportunity for all.

9. Protection of minority groups against discrimination. '

lO. Removal of legal and administrative discriminations against women.

11. A system of federal, state, and local taxation which is ﬂexible and equitable.

.12. Fiscal and monetary policies that promote a stable and expanding economy.

. Responsibility of government to share in the solution of social and economic problems which affect the general

welfare. .

l4. Adequate ﬁnancing of government functions and services.

15. Conservation and development of natural resources in the public interest.

16. Domestic policies which facilitate the solution of international problems.

17. Cooperation with other nations in solving international problems and promoting world peace.

19. Development of international organization and international law to achieve permanent means of cooperation.

 




EXPLANATION OF CURRENT AGENDA—1956-1958

INDIVIDUAL LIBERTIES: Evaluation of the
federal loyalty-security programs, with
recognition of the need for safeguarding
national security and protecting individual
liberties.

The League will ﬁrst attempt to develop a frame-
Work in which to evaluate the loyalty-security pro-
grams. A look will be taken at varying concepts of
both national security and individual liberties, as well
as at some of the League’s own principles of govern-
ment: representative government and individual lib-
erty; clearly ﬁxed lines of responsibility; governmental
efﬁciency and economy; a merit system for selection,
retention, and promotion of personnel; and domestic
policies which facilitate the solution of international
problems.

Scrutinizing the Programs

With this background, the League will examine and
compare the evolution and current operation of the
ﬁve federal loyalty-security programs: Government
Personnel, which covers virtually all federal em-
ployees; Atomic Energy, which covers Atomic Energy

Commission employees as well as employees of in- .

dustries with atomic contracts; Military Personnel,
Which covers the uniformed personnel of the Army,
Navy, and Air Force; Port Security, under the U. S.
Coast Guard, which covers seamen and dockworkers;
and Industrial Security, which covers employees of
industries with defense contracts.

Emphasis will be placed on the relative effectiveness
and fairness of the programs, as well as their direct
and indirect impact at home and abroad.

Consideration also will be given to the role which
each of the three branches of government has had in
shaping these programs. This kind of scrutiny of
security operations should offer opportunities for the
League to learn more about the way in which the
federal government operates.

The League also will examine differing reactions to
the programs and some of the concrete proposals for
their improvement to be made by government agencies
such as the Commission on Government Security and
by nongovernmental groups such as the New York
City Bar Association.

Asking the Right Questions

At this point the League should be ready to con-
sider how the programs measure up to security-liberty
needs, as well as to the League’s government principles.
The earlier review of principles should have provided
many different kinds of questions, covering possible
loopholes in security protection, overlapping responsi-
bilities, duplication in efforts, or inequities in treat-
ment of individuals:

Are the programs well administered?

Are the programs producing a maximum amOunt of
security with a minimum of cost?

Are the programs in line with American traditions
of justice and fair play?

In evaluating the programs and the proposals for
improvement, the League will move in the direction
of possible agreement on a position. The Convention
indicated a strong desire to take action as soon as
consensus has developed within the League, perhaps
by the time the Council meets in April 1957.

CONSERVATION: Study of water resources

The study of water resources will be directed toward
ﬁnding answers to four broad questions:

1) What is the role of the federal government in
the conservation and distribution of water supply?

2) Are present methods used by the federal govern-
ment measuring up to League standards of good gov-
ernment, such as: clearly ﬁxed responsibility; coor-
dination of federal, state, and local governments;
economy and efﬁciency in government; and manage-
ment of natural resources in the public interest?

3) What are the essentials of a sound U. S. water
policy?

4) What problems are likely to arise in carrying out
a federal program locally and regionally, and how can
they best be solved in the areas of planning, ﬁnancing
and management?

The national study, as planned, will include such
problems as stream pollution, watershed and forest
management, ﬂood control, and soil conservation.
Which federal agencies are concerned with these areas,
and how they cooperate, will be the basis of the study.

Material Already Available

There is a great deal of reliable factual material in
print which League members will ﬁnd useful in the
early stages of their work on conservation. Three
Presidential Commissions have studied U. S. water
resources and reported their ﬁndings and possible
solutions, in 1950, 1952, and 1955. Water resource
task forces of both the First and Second Hoover Com-
missions made exhaustive studies. The report of the
Mid-Century Conference on Resources for the Future,
published in 1953, contains pertinent material. The
Department of Agriculture yearbooks for 1948, 1949,
and 1955, entitled “Trees,” “Grass,” and “Water,”
respectively, contain uSeful references.

The state Leagues will have a key role in develop-
ing this new study. Their assignment will include
identifying the federal agencies engaged in water re-
source work within the state and relating the federal
programs to those of state and local agencies. These
intergovernmental aspects of the League study were of
paramount interest to League members 1n the selection
of this subject for the Program, both in the program
proposals and during debate at Convention.

Some states have already examined their own struc-
ture of government in this ﬁeld, but to most it will be
a new project. Any regional approach by the Leagues
to the solution of water resource problems will be
delayed until the broad national study has resulted in
some general areas of agreement.

Reports from Council delegates next year will deter-
mine how near the Leagues are to arriving at con-
sensus in some areas, and whether action can be
included during the second year.

of millions of animals in slaughterhouses. Obviously‘
this is a matter that must be dealt with.sometime.

This is merely a note to tell you that I have been
unable to contemplate a program.on the subject up to
this time because of other commitments.

Sincerely yours,

Norman Cousins,
Editor
September 25, 1956

Rﬁss Christine Stevens
Secretary-Treasurer

Society for Animal Protective
Legislation

745 Fifth Avenue, Room 812
New York 22, New York

Dear Miss Stevens:

my apologies for the delay in
thanking you for your letter and for the
material on humane slaughter. The com-
bination of summer vacations and the
moving of our offices have put me far
behind in my correspondence.

It was good of you to write me in
such detail. I am in complete sympathy
with your cause. I am afraid that the
subject may be somewhat afield for the
Saturday Review but we will give it our
consideration.

Sincerely,

June 1, 1957

Dear Mimi:

I am sorry that I didn't get to see you while I was in

ew York for a couple of days last week, but I have
just returned from South America and have been ex-
tremely busy. I am looking forward to seeing you,
however, when I come down again during the latter
part of this month.

I am attaching a letter from my friend, Senator Cape-
hart, acknoWledging my letter to him regarding the hu-
mane slaughter of animals. I have also written to sev-
eral other senators and congressmen, and wanted to let
you know that I am doing everything I possibly can.

How is my friend Ludwig? Give him my love when you
write him.

Looking forward to seeing you soon, I am

Fondly,

%

Mrs. Ludwig Bemelmans

June 28, 1957

Mr. D. Collis Wager
Attorney At law
Mayra Building
Uties , N.Y.

Dear Collis,

The Committee ohihgrieulture this morning passed out
of Committee HR 8308, a bill establishing humane slaughter
methods for livestock. The bill now goes to the Rules Committee.
What that Committee-maydo with the bill is somewhat uncertain.

The.Agriueltural,Committee will ask for en “Open
rule" wheih provides that the bill in question ean e amended at
any point. Material now in lit the bill can be struck from.the
bill; new material added to it or dates that are now in the bill
can be changed.

Because-of the “open rule" that will apply to this
legislation when it eomes to the floor of the ngse, the bill
passed out of the.Agrieulture Committee by an unanimous vote.

Again may I say that I will vote for this legislation
if and when it comes to the floor of the House.

Sincerely yours,

Bill
William R. Williams, M.C.

June 26, 1957.

Mr. D. Collie Wager,
Attorney at Lwa,
Mayro Bldgo,

Utica, New York.

Dear Collie : -

Replying to your letter under date of June 21 which again expresses
your interest in a question of hmaane slaughter legislation.
1

I have received noticd from Congressman Cooley, Chairman of the Committee
on Agriculture, that an executive session of the full committee is scheduled for
Friday morning, June the 28th, for the consideration of Humane Slaughter legislation.
Notice provides that final action is expected to be taken on this matter at this
scheduled session.

I understand that Congressman Poage, Chaimuan of the sub-committee on live-
stock, has prepared a clean bill, or, in other words, an entirely new bill on this
subject. The Poage bill, along with other bills formerly introduced, will be dis-i
cussed at this executive session. I have no way of knowing what the new Poage bill may
contain.

Personally, I believe everyone is favorable toward a methof of humane '
slaughter for all animals. However, be cause of differences in opinion, is is found
that this particular legislation has become complicated and somewhat controversial.
It is pointed out that it is most difficult to legislate a universal program for
the slaughtering of livestock for food purposes, and, as you 1mm, there is con-a
sideration and mnemptions given certain segments who commonly slaughter livestock
for food purposes. Some. people question the constitutionality of this kind of
legislation.

Referring to your comment regarding the Department of Agriculture and Ezra
Benson as a stumbling block in opposition to the legislation, I believe it is unfair
and without foundation of fact. As you. already know, the Department of Agriculture
has no power to legislate, nor does it at any time have power under the law to lobby
for or against any legislation. The Department of Agriculture carries out the
direction of Congress aslaws provide for Agriculture. It has very little ﬂexibility
on its own.

Permit to say Congressman Poage is Chairman o‘i‘ the subcommittee on livestock.
His failure to report on the Hmnane Slaughter Logislation is his personal responsibility.

I have already advised you relative to my personal views and position regarding
the legislation. I will support it in Committee and vote for it if it should come to
the floor of the House. '

In conclusion, it should be understood that the Democrat majority is in
complete control of this 85th Congress. The majority they enjOy on every Comittee
and in the House, places themsponsibility for passing or defeating all legislation,
and is a party responsibility. I could point out that the majority party always
makes a special effort to make clear to the public that they are responsible for every
legislation passed by Congress that is to the advantage of the people and good for the

country.
I will advise you immediately after the executive session on Friday, relative to
the WW action of the whole committee on this legislation. '

Sincerely yours,

Siam-2d William R Williams, M. c.

Dear Mrs. Stevens:

You asked for a report on the at-
titude of our Florida Congressmen concerning"
humane slaughter legislation.

The following men have assured us that they
will ggggwwﬁor. and support the passage of
this legislation:

Congressmen from Florida
Charles E.Bennett
A.S.H6P10ﬂg,JP.'
D.R.Mathews.

The following men we are not sure of:
Congressmen from Florida ‘ ‘
William C.Cramer

Robert L.Sykes

Dante B.Foscell

Paul G.Bogers

James A.Haley.

I have just written to our I6 societies asking
them to send telegrams and letters to support
H.B. 8308 and this Federation will again send
teﬁegrams to each of the eight Florida Congress-
men and also letters on this stationery. I think
home Opinions help.

Thank you again for all that you are doing to
help this work.

July 2,1957

Sincerely yours,

Erie, Pa., July ll, 1957

Mrs. Christine Stevens, Secretary
Society for Animal Protective Legislation
7H5 Fifth Avenue
New York 22, N. Y.

Re: H.R. 8308
Dear Mrs. Stevens:

As requested in your form letter of
June 29, I have submitted the news release
regarding humane slaughter to our local editor
which he used in the Times editorial column
as per the enclosed clipping.

I also wrote to our Congressman, the
Hon. Carroll D. Kearns, as well as the Hon.
Agustine Bernard Kelley and Hon. Leon H. Gavin,
and this morning received a reply from Mr.
Gavin. He expressed appreciation, saying it
was helpful to-know how we are thinking on
any proposed legislation before the Congress.
He promised "careful attention and consideration“
when (and if) Bill H.R. 8308 is presented to
the H0118 ea

In addition, I wrote to the twelve
members of the Rules Committee.

I do hope for the best possible outcome.
I feel very deeply on this whole matter.

Sincerely,

,Ibvu4wua

Lenore Deck.

Facts Cited By Meat Institute
0n Humane Slaughter “Distortedﬁ
ﬂy Mrs. James V. Griffith.

In rebuttal to the article, published in
your paper April 29, by the American Meat
institute regarding the slaughter legisla-
tion now pending, I must comment that the

facts were distorted. The institute claimed -

that it is impossible to adopt such humane
til-nethods for the slaughtering of food ani-
mals here; that it is more expensive to do in
this country than it is in Europe, and that
the price of meat to the consumer would be-
come higher, if humane methods were used.

The following companies are anesthetiz-
ing animals through the use of carbon di-
iixldG, electricity and the captive-bolt pistol,
:ée'z‘idering them unconscious before they are
tuned: George A. Hormel & Co., Oscar Mayer
x. Co., the Seitz Packing C0,, C. A. Swanson
:3: Sons and a few lesser companies.

A Hormel executive told a Senate com-
mittee that his company’s labor costs were
a‘g-«zgtua.lly decreased by using their humane
method. The president of the Seitz Packing
ilo. told the American Meat Institute: “The
a:::~aptive-'bol.t pistol is so easy to use and so
effective that we have no desire to go back
to the old hammer method. Our results are
better, our operation more effecient. Our
end product is improved—and we’re slaught—
ering cattle in a much more humane fash-.
1011.”

Oscar Mayer & Co. for years has been
using the captive-boltpistol (which makes
cattle instantly unconscious before being
shackled, hoisted or cut). This pistol com-
pletely eliminates the brutalities of the
sledge hammer.

This should give the lie to the Amer-
ican Meat Institute’s claims. Fifty million
dollars is wasted by the packing industry
each. year because of bruised meat!

Fair Lawn, N. J.

 




l5-46‘Eastern Drive
Fair Lawn, n. J.

May 28, 1957

Society for Animal Protective Legislation,
745 Fifth Ave.,
New York 22, Ne Ye

Gentlemen:-

Will you kindly advise me whether-or not Congress has
yet acted on the slaughter legislation now pending? I have been
watching the papers, but have seen no report on it thus far.

It has only been within the last month.that I learned
of the inhumane slaughter of our food animals. I was shocked be-
yond words, as I had always believed that death to the animals
was instantaneous and humane. I got busy right away, and have been
busy ever since. In two weeks I typed up about 100 cards Which.were
given along with pamphlets.to our families, and friends, our former
minister, who is also a good friend, and neighbors. They all signed
the cards and sent them out. I have had correspondence with our
Congressman, Senators, etc., and have written several newspapers.

I am enclosing a letter published by the New York World Telegram

and Sun on.May 10, 1957. I only mention my efforts because I know
omwdikz

it willwheartening reading to your group to know that the word is

spreading, and as it spreads, peOple are doing something about it.

I would greatly appreciate your letting me know the la-
test news on what is going to be done for our animal friends. I
understand that the bills were to have been voted upon this month,
but as yet have read nothing about the subject.

I am enclosing a self addressed, stamped enveIOpe for

your convenience. With all good wishes to you for your wonderful

work, and thanking you,
I am

, Sincerely,
/ ,277w L/I VWMZ/

UNITED STATES DEPARTMENT OF AGRICULTURE

AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH SERVICE
INFORMATION DIVISION
WASHINGTON 25, D. c.

August 16, 1957

Miss.Alice E. Parkins
Route 3, Box 2u5_02
Vancouve r ,- Washington

Dear Miss Parkins:

This is in response to your communication concerning

legislation dealing with the use of humane methods in the

slaughter of livestock and poultry.

The Department favors humane slaughter by any method
that is found to be both humane and practical. We recom-
mend the enactment of either H. R. 5820 or S. l213, both
of which provide for an orderly approach to this objec-
tive. In addition, these bills provide for a coordinated
national effort to improve the humane handling and trans-
port of livestock and poultry during the marketing process
from farm to slaughter.

Sincerely yours,

gm

E. G. Mbore, Director

RICHARD ASHTON HUTCHINSON
SOUTH BEND, INDIANA

June 17, 1957

Dear Mimi:

I am endeavoring to reach our local congressman, Nimtz,
on the phone and will also get ahold of the Republican boss
for the State and ask him if he will have a little pressure
brought to bear on Congressman Ralph Harvey.

I just talked to Nimtz' secretary, and he is in Washington,
so I have put in a call for him and will ask him to contact
Harvey, too.

I am attaching hereto a copy of a letter from Senator W. E.
Jenner of Indiana. When I talked to him over the phone he

told me that when it comes up in the Senate he will boost it
along.

Pat and Yves, the chap she is engaged to, and Dickie and
June Adams, the girl he is engaged to, leave this afternoon
to return to New York. I have had a lot of fun with them as
there have been many parties. The house will be rather
quiet when they leave.

I talked to Mary this morning, and she is fine. She says
that the weather is a bit warm, but that her room is nice

and cool.

Give my best to Ludwig when you write. I am looking forward
to seeing you both soon.

With love to all the Bemelmans, I am,

Fondly,

\

._ ‘_i_.___.._.-\.i\§

Mrs. Ludwig Bemelmans
10 E. 78th Street
New York, N. Y. ‘

UNITED STATES SENATE
Committee on Finance

4 June 1957

Mr. Richard A. Hutchinson
Studebaker-Packard Corporation
South Bend 27, Indiana

Dear Mr. Hutchinson:

I want to thank you kindly for your recent communication concerning
legislation to force all slaughter houses to use some form of anesthetic.

Numerous bills on this subject have been introduced in the House and the
Senate and have been referred to the Agriculture Committees of both
Houses for study. The House Committee has held hearings on this sub-
ject but it is too early, of course, to even predict what recommendations
the Committee will make when this study has been completed. I can assure
you that I shall follow closely the progress of the legislation and it will
have my most careful attention when it is before the Senate.

48'

X!
I appreciate hearing from you and with kindest regards and best
wishes, I am .

Sinc e rely your 5

(Signed) W. E. Jenner
